<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41597"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The tea plant reference genome and improved gene annotation using long-read and paired-end sequencing data"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Scientific Data 2019 6:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-07-15"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Tea is a globally consumed non-alcohol beverage with great economic importance. However, lack of the reference genome has largely hampered the utilization of precious tea plant genetic resources towards breeding. To address this issue, we previously generated a high-quality reference genome of tea plant using Illumina and PacBio sequencing technology, which produced a total of 2,124&#8201;Gb short and 125&#8201;Gb long read data, respectively. A hybrid strategy was employed to assemble the tea genome that has been publicly released. We here described the data framework used to generate, annotate and validate the genome assembly. Besides, we re-predicted the protein-coding genes and annotated their putative functions using more comprehensive omics datasets with improved training models. We reassessed the assembly and annotation quality using the latest version of BUSCO. These data can be utilized to develop new methodologies/tools for better assembly of complex genomes, aid in finding of novel genes, variations and evolutionary clues associated with tea quality, thus help to breed new varieties with high yield and better quality in the future.

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Tea is a globally consumed non-alcohol beverage with great economic importance. However, lack of the reference genome has largely hampered the utilization of precious tea plant genetic resources towards breeding. To address this issue, we previously generated a high-quality reference genome of tea plant using Illumina and PacBio sequencing technology, which produced a total of 2,124âGb short and 125âGb long read data, respectively. A hybrid strategy was employed to assemble the tea genome that has been publicly released. We here described the data framework used to generate, annotate and validate the genome assembly. Besides, we re-predicted the protein-coding genes and annotated their putative functions using more comprehensive omics datasets with improved training models. We reassessed the assembly and annotation quality using the latest version of BUSCO. These data can be utilized to develop new methodologies/tools for better assembly of complex genomes, aid in finding of novel genes, variations and evolutionary clues associated with tea quality, thus help to breed new varieties with high yield and better quality in the future.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Abs2" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content">
                <div class="c-article-table-container"><div class="c-article-table-border c-table-scroll-wrapper"><div class="c-table-scroll-wrapper__content" data-component-scroll-wrapper=""><table class="data last-table"><tbody><tr><td class="u-text-left ">

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Background &amp; Summary"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Background &amp; Summary</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Tea is the oldest and most prevalent nonalcoholic beverage in the world<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Banerjee, B. In Tea: Cultivation to consumption (eds Willson, K. C. &amp; Clifford, M. N.) 25â51 (Springer Netherlands, 1992)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d26373e926">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mondal, T. K., Bhattacharya, A., Laxmikumaran, M. &amp; Ahuja, P. S. Recent advances of tea (Camellia sinensis) biotechnology. Plant Cell, Tissue and Organ Culture 76, 195â254 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d26373e929">2</a></sup>. It is made from the cured leaves of tea plant (<i>Camellia sinensis</i>), an important economic crop planted worldwide. Tea harbors rich characteristic compounds (e.g. tea polyphenol, theanine and caffeine) that are beneficial to the human body and can effectively prevent the occurrence of malignant tumors and reduce the occurrence of cardiovascular and cerebrovascular diseases and diseases of the nervous system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Chung, F.-L., Schwartz, J., Herzog, C. R. &amp; Yang, Y.-M. Tea and cancer prevention: studies in animals and humans. The Journal of Nutrition 133, 3268â3274 (2003)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d26373e936">3</a></sup>. At present, nearly 3 billion people in more than 160 countries drink tea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mondal, T. K., Bhattacharya, A., Laxmikumaran, M. &amp; Ahuja, P. S. Recent advances of tea (Camellia sinensis) biotechnology. Plant Cell, Tissue and Organ Culture 76, 195â254 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d26373e940">2</a></sup>. This makes tea an important source of economic income for the worldâs major tea producing countries. With more than 18 billion cups of tea consumed daily<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Marcos, A., Fisher, A., Rea, G. &amp; Hill, S. J. Preliminary study using trace element concentrations and a chemometrics approach to determine the geographical origin of tea. Journal of Analytical Atomic Spectrometry 13, 521â525 (1998)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d26373e944">4</a></sup>, tea is now commercially cultivated on more than 4.10 million hectares of land on a continent-wide scale, and 5.95 million metric tons of tea worldwide were produced annually in 2016.</p><p>The tea plant primarily includes two varieties namely: <i>C</i>. <i>sinensis</i> var. <i>sinensis</i> (Chinese type tea; CSS) and <i>C</i>. <i>sinensis</i> var. <i>assamica</i> (Assam type tea; CSA). The Chinese type tea accounts for over 80% of tea production worldwide and is suitable for the manufacture of six major teas. It has a broader and distinct geographical distribution from the Assam tea that is predominantly distributed in the southwest of China and the Assam region of northeast India<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Ming, T. &amp; Bartholomew, B. Theaceae. Flora of China 12, 366â478 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d26373e970">5</a></sup>. Over the past 10 years, we have made a series of attempts to gain a fundamental understanding of the genetic basis of tea qualityâan essential question that has puzzled the tea scientific community and seriously hindered the sustainable development of the tea industry for more than 50 years. We therefore initiated a joint collaborative project to generate a reference-quality genome assembly for the Chinese type tea plant<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e974">6</a></sup>. In comparison with the previously published CSA genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Xia, E. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Molecular Plant 10, 866â877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d26373e978">7</a></sup>, we found that the two varieties of tea plants (CSS and CSA) diverged from their common ancestor ~0.38â1.54 million years ago. Tea plant undergoes two whole genome duplication events that facilitate the expansion of various genes associated with the biosynthesis of secondary metabolites that determine tea quality. Most importantly, we identified and functionally validated a gene involved in theanine biosynthesis. These results and datasets provide a solid foundation for the tea community to uncover the genetic basis of tea quality and genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Xia, E. et al. Tea Plant Information Archive (TPIA): A comprehensive genomics and bioinformatics platform for tea plant. Plant Biotechnology Journal, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1111/pbi.13111&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d26373e982">8</a></sup>.</p><p>In this descriptor, we mainly described the plant material and full data sets generated and used to assemble, annotate and validate the tea plant reference genome: (1) raw Illumina whole genome sequencing (WGS) data for genome assembly; (2) raw PacBio sequencing data for genome assembly; (3) raw PacBio RNA sequencing data from mixed tissues of tea plant for gene annotation; (4) eighteen bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs) and BAC end sequences used for quality validation of genome assembly; and (5) the final assembly and latest release of reference genome of tea plant. We reassessed the quality of genome assembly by using datasets from the latest version of BUSCO. Additionally, we re-annotated the protein-coding genes and predicted their putative functions using more comprehensive omics datasets and improved <i>ab intio</i> prediction models. The described sequencing data and newly released annotations in this study will not only help computational biologist to test the novel methodologies and tools to assemble the complex tree genomes, but also facilitate the tea community to better understand the genetic basis of tea quality and genome evolution.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Selection of the target tea plant individual for genome sequencing</h3><p>Interspecific hybridization is an extensive phenomenon in tea plant, which causes large difficulties in the genome assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Chen, L., Zhou, Z.-X. &amp; Yang, Y.-J. Genetic improvement and breeding of tea plant (Camellia sinensis) in China: from individual selection to hybridization and molecular breeding. Euphytica 154, 239â248 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1004">9</a></sup>. In order to select the most appropriate and suitable tea plant material for sequencing, we collected a total of 18 tea plants from different locations of China that represents the majority of tea cultivars and wild relatives<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Yang, H. et al. Genetic divergence between Camellia sinensis and its wild relatives revealed via genome-wide SNPs from RAD sequencing. PLoS One 11, e0151424, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0151424&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1008">10</a></sup>. Fresh leaves of each individual plant were used for DNA extraction. All individuals were genotyped to estimate the degree of heterozygosity using the high-throughput genome-wide restriction site-associated DNA sequencing (RAD-Seq) technology. Only the bi-allelic SNPs were retained and used for subsequent analysis. The heterozygosity rate was estimated by the ratio of the numbers of heterozygous SNPs to the total length of the shared SNP-associated genome fragments<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Yang, H. et al. Genetic divergence between Camellia sinensis and its wild relatives revealed via genome-wide SNPs from RAD sequencing. PLoS One 11, e0151424, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0151424&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1012">10</a></sup>. Results showed that the heterozygosity rate of the investigated tea plants ranged from 0.0016 to 0.0081 with an average of 0.0032 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Fig1">1</a>). We found that the commercial variety âShuchazaoâ (Accession: GS2002008) exhibits a comparatively low level of heterozygosity (2.7â°) among the Chinese type tea cultivars tested, and therefore was selected for genome sequencing. Shuchazao is among the most widely grown tea plants in China with various excellent agricultural traits (e.g. early sprouting, highly resistance to cold and drought, and high yields).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0127-1/MediaObjects/41597_2019_127_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0127-1/MediaObjects/41597_2019_127_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="728" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Evaluation of the heterozygosity of 18 representative tea plants using RAD-seq for selection of individuals to genome sequencing. The left panel indicates the accession names of tea plant species/varieties. The F1 individual was a hybrid from âYunkang #10Â Ã Fudingdabaichaâ. The middle panel shows the heterozygosity dynamics among different tea plants. Orange bar represents wild tea plants, while blue and green bars characterize semi-wild and cultivated tea plants, respectively. The right panel indicates species name. The heterozygous data of each tea plant was collected from our previous work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1029">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Yang, H. et al. Genetic divergence between Camellia sinensis and its wild relatives revealed via genome-wide SNPs from RAD sequencing. PLoS One 11, e0151424, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0151424&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1032">10</a></sup>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Plant material, DNA extraction, library construction and Illumina sequencing</h3><p>Plant material was grown under field conditions at 916 tea plantation of Shucheng, Anhui province, China. Tender shoots were harvested and stored at â80âÂ°C prior to DNA extraction. Cetyltrimethyl ammonium bromide (CTAB) method was employed to isolate the genomic DNA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Murray, M. G. &amp; Thompson, W. F. Rapid isolation of high molecular weight plant DNA. Nucleic Acids Research 8, 4321â4326 (1980)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1052">11</a></sup>. A total of twenty paired-end libraries, including ten short-insert (170âbp, 250âbp, 500âbp and 800âbp) and ten long-insert libraries (2âkb, 5âkb, 10âkb, 20âkb and 40âkb), were prepared separately following the Illuminaâs protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1056">6</a></sup>. At least 5âÎ¼g of genomic DNA was used for the construction of small-insert paired-end and approximately 10â30âÎ¼g was used to prepare large-insert mate-pair library. The libraries were sequenced using Illumina Hiseq 2500 platform. In total, approximate 2,124âGb of raw sequencing data, accounting ~699-fold coverage of whole genome, was generated (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab1">1</a>). The data from short-insert (&lt;1âkb) and long-insert libraries (â¥2âkb) were 1,262âGb and 862âGb, respectively.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of genome sequencing data of tea plant using Illumina and PacBio SMRT sequencing platforms.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Illumina reads preprocessing and genome properties</h3><p>Similar to the procedures described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1797">6</a></sup>, the raw sequencing data was preprocessed to remove adapter contaminations, PCR duplicates and sequencing errors. Briefly, (1) we removed the reads from short and long insert-size libraries if they separately contain more than 2% and 5% of unidentified bases (Ns); (2) we removed the reads from short and long insert-size libraries if they host more than 40% and 30% of low quality bases (phred score â¤Â 7), respectively; (3) we trimmed the adapter in the reads by aligning them to adapter sequence (allowing â¤Â 3âbp mismatch); (4) we removed the reads derived from PCR duplication if two paired-end reads were completely identical; (5) we aligned the sequencing reads against NCBI NR database and removed the potential contaminations derived from known bacteria or viruses. After filtering, we obtained a total of 1,325âGb of high-quality reads, covering approximately ~436-fold of tea plant genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>The genome properties of tea plant were characterized by performing the <i>k</i>-mer analysis of sequencing data from short-insert libraries using Jellyfish<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1810">12</a></sup>. A <i>k</i>-mer refers to an oligonucleotide of <i>k</i> bp in length. The bimodal distribution of the 17-mer indicates that tea plant harbors a heterozygous diploid genome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Fig2">2</a>) with the homozygous peak located at 86Ã and the heterozygous peak was found at 43Ã. Notably, compared to the homozygous peak on the right, the sharp heterozygous peak on the left indicates a high level of genome heterozygosity. As proposed by Liu and colleagues<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Liu, B. et al. Estimation of genomic characteristics by analyzing k-mer frequency in de novo genome projects. Preprint at, &#xA;                    https://arxiv.org/abs/1308.2012&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2013)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1824">13</a></sup>, the genome size can be basically inferred from the total number of <i>k</i>-mers divided by the <i>k</i>-mer depth. This estimated the genome size of tea plant to be 2.93âGb (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Fig2">2</a>), which is quite consistent with the estimation from flow cytometry (2.98âGb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1837">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Xia, E. et al. The tea tree genome provides insights into tea flavor and independent evolution of caffeine biosynthesis. Molecular Plant 10, 866â877 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1840">7</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0127-1/MediaObjects/41597_2019_127_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0127-1/MediaObjects/41597_2019_127_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="507" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The 17-mer distribution used for the estimation of genome size of tea plant. The distribution of 17-mer was calculated using jellyfish based on the sequencing data from short insert size libraries (insert sizeâ=â500âbp). The heterozygous and homozygous peaks of read depth were marked, suggesting a high complexity of tea plant genome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">PacBio single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing</h3><p>Two types of PacBio libraries, including 10âkb and 20âkb library, were constructed for sequencing using PacBio RSII platform. For 10âkb libraries, the raw sequencing data was generated from 44 SMRT cells and further filtered using the RS_Subreads protocol (i.e., minimum subread lengthâ=â2âkb, minimum polymerase read qualityâ=â0.8), resulting in a total of 33.2âGb (~11-fold coverage) useable data (total number of subreadsâ=â6.2âM reads, mean subread lengthâ=â5.3âkb, subread N50â=â6.4âkb). For 20âkb library, data from 97 SMRT cells were treated as described above, yielding in a total of 92.2âGb (~30-fold coverage) data (total number of subreadsâ=â10.6âM reads, mean subread lengthâ=â8.7âkb, subread N50â=â12.6âkb). The obtained reads were then further corrected by using the Pacbio corrected reads pipeline (PBcR). This exercise finally yielded a total of 86.4âGb high quality subreads for subsequent data analysis (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome assembly using illumina and PacBio sequencing data</h3><p>We employed a hybrid approach that integrates Illumina paired-end and PacBio long-read sequencing data to assemble the tea plant genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1881">6</a></sup>. We <i>de novo</i> assembled the high-quality Illumina sequencing reads into contigs and scaffolds using SOAP<i>denovo</i> and Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Research 24, 1384â1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1891">14</a></sup>. Platanus is an effective <i>de novo</i> assembler for highly heterozygous genomes. Briefly, we removed the sequencing reads with 17-mer frequency â¤Â 10 and merged paired-end reads from two libraries (170âbp &amp; 250âbp) by sequence overlap, independently. This resulted in a total of 553âGb of clean short read data used for contig construction. The contigs were constructed using SOAP<i>denovo</i>. Low coverage links and bubble structures caused by heterozygosity or errors were removed. All clean reads from small-insert and large-insert size libraries were aligned onto the preassembled contigs. According to the order and distance information, the assembled contigs were further elongated and eventually combined into scaffolds using Platanus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Research 24, 1384â1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1902">14</a></sup>.</p><p>We closed the gaps that might be repeat sequences masked during the construction of scaffolds using Krskgf and Gapclose<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Li, R. et al. SOAP2: an improved ultrafast tool for short read alignment. Bioinformatics 25, 1966â1967 (2009)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1909">15</a></sup>. Briefly, all paired-end sequencing reads were first mapped onto the assembled scaffolds, and then those read pairs with one read well-aligned on the contigs and another located in the gap region were retrieved and assembled locally to close the gaps. To fill the gap as much as possible, we further applied Pbjelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS One 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1913">16</a></sup> to close remaining gaps within scaffolds using PacBio long-read sequencing data from 10âkb and 20âkb libraries.</p><p>Haplomerger<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Huang, S. et al. HaploMerger: reconstructing allelic relationships for polymorphic diploid genome assemblies. Genome Research 22, 1581â1588 (2012)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d26373e1920">17</a></sup> was used for merging and truncating of scaffolds originating from heterozygous genomic regions. The resulting final assembly of tea plant genome contains 2.89âGb contigs (N50â=â67.07âkb) and 3.14âGb scaffolds (N50â=â1.39âMb) (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab2">2</a>). The largest scaffold was observed 7.31âMb in length.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-3"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Statistics of the tea plant genome assembly and improved annotation.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/tables/3"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genome annotation</h3><p>We used a combined method that integrates <i>ab inito</i> gene prediction, homolog searching and EST/unigene-based prediction to re-annotate the protein-coding genes in the tea plant genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2392">6</a></sup>. (1) We applied Augustus and SNAP to perform <i>de novo</i> gene prediction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. Augustus: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Research 32, W309âW312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2399">18</a></sup>. Compared to the previous annotation, there are two improvements in this step. We improved the accuracy and sensitivity of the prediction model applied by Augustus through iteratively self-training with full-length transcripts of tea plant. These full-length transcripts were generated by Pacbio sequencing of eight developmental tissues: apical buds, young leaves, mature leaves, old leaves, immature stems, flowers, young fruits, and tender roots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xu, Q. et al. Transcriptome profiling using single-molecule direct RNA sequencing approach for in-depth understanding of genes in secondary metabolism pathways of Camellia sinensis. Frontiers in Plant Science 8, 1205 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2403">19</a></sup>. Moreover, we incorporated gene predictions from SNAP, a fast and widely used gene predictor for both eukaryotic and prokaryotic gene annotation. <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> was set as the training organism for SNAP gene model prediction. In total, 52,987 and 126,067 gene models are annotated by Augustus and SNAP gene predictors, respectively. (2) We performed homology-based gene predictions by using the homologous sequences from proteomes of kiwifruit (<i>Actinidia chinensis</i>), coffee (<i>Coffea canephora</i>), poplar (<i>Populus trichocarpa</i>) and grape (<i>Vitis vinifera</i>), as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2423">6</a></sup>. Briefly, the homologous protein sequences were first mapped to the masked assembly of tea plant genome using TBLASTN. BLAST hits were filtered by E-value (threshold 1e<sup>â5</sup>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Research 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2430">20</a></sup>. The homologous genomic fragments of the target genes together with their 3âkb flanking sequences were then extracted and fed to GeneWise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and genomewise. Genome Research 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2434">21</a></sup> to define gene models using the matching proteins. This predicted a total of 59,739, 42,217, 65,800, and 40,491 tea plant gene models from kiwifruit, coffee, poplar, and grape protein alignments, respectively. (3) We performed the EST-based gene prediction by aligning a total of 26,046 tea ESTs from GenBank to genome assembly using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Kent, W. J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Research 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2438">22</a></sup>. The alignments with identity â¥90% and coverage â¥90% were retained and further fed to PASA (Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments) software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the program to assemble spliced alignments. Genome Biology 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2442">23</a></sup> to predict a total of 26,318 gene models. Besides, the aforementioned RNA-seq data from eight tissues was assembled using the StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Pertea, M. et al. StringTie enables improved reconstruction of a transcriptome from RNA-seq reads. Nature Biotechnology 33, 290â295 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2446">24</a></sup>, generating a total of 61,681 gene models. (4) The gene models generated from different prediction methods were combined into a consensus gene set using MAKER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Holt, C. &amp; Yandell, M. MAKER2: an annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 491 (2011)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2451">25</a></sup> with further filtering, yielding a total of 53,512 protein-coding genes in tea plant genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab2">2</a>). The average gene length is 3,747âbp with an average intron length of 712âbp.</p><p>We predicted the putative functions of the 53,512 tea plant genes using various public well-known protein databases (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Fig3">3a</a>). We searched homologous of the tea plant genes in the Swiss-prot protein databases using BLASTP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Altschul, S. F. et al. Gapped BLAST and PSI-BLAST: a new generation of protein database search programs. Nucleic Acids Research 25, 3389â3402 (1997)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2464">20</a></sup> with an E-value threshold of â¤10<sup>â5</sup>. In total, the function of 34,694 (64.83%) genes are annotated (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab2">2</a>). We used InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScanâan integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847â848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2473">26</a></sup> to detect conserved domains and assign gene ontology (GO) terms to tea plant genes. Results show that a total of 39,889 and 21,961 genes can be separately assigned with domains and GO terms. We aligned the tea plant genes against <i>A</i>. <i>thaliana</i> protein datasets (version 10), and found that 38,952 tea plant genes can be allocated functions. Furthermore, we functionally classified the tea plant genes using EuKaryotic Orthologous Groups (KOG). Results demonstrated that a total of 14,587 (27.26%) genes could be annotated with KOG functions. In particular, 831 genes were predicted to be involved in secondary metabolite biosynthesis, transport and catabolism (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0127-1/MediaObjects/41597_2019_127_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41597-019-0127-1/MediaObjects/41597_2019_127_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="756" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Functional annotation of the tea plant protein-coding genes. (<b>a</b>) Venn diagram shows the shared and unique annotations among Swiss-prot, PFAM, GO and The <i>Arabidopsis</i> Information Resource (TAIR; version10). (<b>b</b>) Functional classification of tea plant genes using KOG database. The functional categories of KOG are abbreviated. A: RNA processing and modification; B: chromatin structure and dynamics; C: energy production and conversion; D: cell cycle control, cell division, chromosome partitioning; E: amino acid transport and metabolism; F: nucleotide transport and metabolism; G: carbohydrate transport and metabolism; H: coenzyme transport and metabolism; I: lipid transport and metabolism; J: translation, ribosomal structure and biogenesis; K: transcription; L: replication, recombination and repair; M: cell wall/membrane/envelope biogenesis; N: cell motility; O: posttranslational modification, protein turnover, chaperones; P: inorganic ion transport and metabolism; Q: secondary metabolites biosynthesis, transport and catabolism; R: general function prediction only; S: function unknown; T: signal transduction mechanisms; U: intracellular trafficking, secretion, and vesicular transport; V: defense mechanisms; Y: nuclear structure; and Z: cytoskeleton.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The transposable elements (TEs) were annotated using a combination of <i>de novo</i> methods and homology-based methodsÂ as describedÂ previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2520">6</a></sup>. We performed <i>de novo</i> prediction using RepeatModeler and LTR-Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_Finder: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Research 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2527">27</a></sup>. The repetitive sequences yielded from these two programs were combined to a non-redundant repeat sequence library. With this library, we scanned the representative sequences in tea plant genome. We also performed the homology-based prediction by using RepeatMasker and RepeatProteinMask based on the repeat library from Repbase database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenetic and Genome Research 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2531">28</a></sup> (Repbase-18.04). TEs annotated from <i>de novo</i> and homology-based methods were combined into a single dataset. The redundancies between them were removed to generate the final TEs. The identified TEs were then clustered into families according to their sequence identity. We also employed Tandem Repeats Finder (TRF)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Research 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2539">29</a></sup> to identify tandem repeats in the tea plant genome. In total, we identified 1.86âGb (64%) of repetitive sequences in tea plant genome (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab2">2</a>), which is comparable toÂ the proportion of TEs identified in Sanger-derived BACs (56.81%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tai, Y. et al. Construction and characterization of a bacterial artificial chromosome library for Camellia sinensis. Tree Genetics &amp; Genomes 13, 89 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2546">30</a></sup>. The above methods are expanded versions of descriptions in our related work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2550">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Yang, H. et al. Genetic divergence between Camellia sinensis and its wild relatives revealed via genome-wide SNPs from RAD sequencing. PLoS One 11, e0151424, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.0151424&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2016)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2553">10</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Data Records"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Data Records</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Raw Illumina sequencing reads are available at the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) Sequence Read Archive (SRA) database under Accession Number SRP099527<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="&#xA;                           NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    http://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP099527&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2566">31</a></sup>. The raw SMRT PacBio whole genome sequencing data can be downloaded from NCBI SRA database under Accession numbers SRR8334869 and SRR8334870<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="&#xA;                           NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    http://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP173745&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2570">32</a></sup>. The raw data of tea plant BAC end sequences are deposited in the NCBI SRA database and can be freely accessed under the Accession Numbers SRP111069<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="&#xA;                           NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    http://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRP111069&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2574">33</a></sup>. The raw data of SMRT sequencing of eight tea plant tissues can be downloaded from NCBI SRA database under Accession Numbers SRX2748122<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="&#xA;                           NCBI Sequence Read Archive, &#xA;                    http://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc.sra:SRX2748122&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2578">34</a></sup>. The final genome assembly and gene annotation are deposited into NCBI GenBank database under the Accession Number SDRB00000000<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="&#xA;                           GenBank, &#xA;                    http://identifiers.org/ncbi/insdc:SDRB00000000&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2582">35</a></sup>. The improved gene prediction (GFF3), coding and peptide sequences (FASTA), and gene functional annotations (TXT) are uploaded into Figshare<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Xia, E. et al. The tea plant reference genome and improved gene annotation using long-read and paired-end sequencing data. Figshare, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.7963820.v1&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2587">36</a></sup>, and can also be accessed from the newly developedÂ Tea Plant Information Archive databaseÂ (TPIA; <a href="http://tpia.teaplant.org">http://tpia.teaplant.org</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Xia, E. et al. Tea Plant Information Archive (TPIA): A comprehensive genomics and bioinformatics platform for tea plant. Plant Biotechnology Journal, &#xA;                    https://doi.org/10.1111/pbi.13111&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (2019)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2598">8</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Technical Validation"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Technical Validation</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>Compared to the previous evaluations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Research 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2610">29</a></sup>, we reassessed the completeness of genome assembly and quality of re-annotated gene models using the latest version of Benchmarking Universal Single-Copy Orthologs (BUSCO)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2614">37</a></sup>. We ran BUSCO using a total of 1,440 orthologous groups from plant lineages. <i>Arabidopsis</i> was employed as model for training during Augustus gene model prediction<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Stanke, M., Steinkamp, R., Waack, S. &amp; Morgenstern, B. Augustus: a web server for gene finding in eukaryotes. Nucleic Acids Research 32, W309âW312 (2004)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d26373e2621">18</a></sup>. The results showed that the majority of the plant core genes/orthologues (95.5%) was present in the tea plant genome assembly (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab3">3</a>). Among them, approximately 92.4% and 3.1% were identified as complete and fragmented, respectively. This indicates a relative complete genome assembly.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-4"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab3" data-test="table-caption">Table 3 Validation of the assembly quality and improved gene annotation of tea plant genome using three methodologies.</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1/tables/4"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>We also used the 1,440 BUSCO conserved genes from plant lineage to assess the quality of gene models re-predicted in this study. Results showed that nearly 94.4% of the plant conserved genes/orthologues can be found in the predicted gene set, evidencing a good quality of the gene annotation. The complete and fragmented BUSCO account for 86.2% and 8.2% of the total plant core gene/orthologue groups (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab3">3</a>).</p><p>As described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e3014">6</a></sup>, we fully sequenced a total of 18 BACs to assess the accuracy of genome assembly. The BACs were randomly selected from a constructed BAC library that consists of 161,280 clones covering approximately 6-fold depth of tea plant genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tai, Y. et al. Construction and characterization of a bacterial artificial chromosome library for Camellia sinensis. Tree Genetics &amp; Genomes 13, 89 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d26373e3018">30</a></sup>. Results show that 98.3% of the sequenced BACs can be well aligned to the genome assembly, showing a high base-level accuracy of the genome assembly (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab3">3</a>). We further employed a total of 24 PCR reactions to investigate the quality of genome assembly. The primers were provided in the previously published tea plant genome paper<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e3025">6</a></sup>. Results showed that, of the 24 primer pairs, 22 (91.67%) could yield approximately same size of PCR fragment, showing an accurate genome assembly at the base resolution (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#Tab3">3</a>).</p><p>These validations are expanded versions of descriptions in our related work<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wei, C. et al. Draft genome sequence of Camellia sinensis var. sinensis provides insights into the evolution of the tea genome and tea quality. Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 115, E4151âE4158 (2018)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d26373e3035">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Tai, Y. et al. Construction and characterization of a bacterial artificial chromosome library for Camellia sinensis. Tree Genetics &amp; Genomes 13, 89 (2017)." href="/articles/s41597-019-0127-1#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d26373e3038">30</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="code-availability" data-title="Code Availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="code-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="code-availability">Code Availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="code-availability-content">
              
              <p>All the bioinformatics tools/packages used in building of tea plant genome are described below along with their versions, settings and parameters.</p>
              <p>(<b>1</b>) <b>Jellyfish</b>: version 2.2.6, k-mer size of 23; (<b>2</b>) <b>RS_Subreads protocol</b>: minimum subread length (equal or larger than 2âkb); minimum polymerase read quality (equal or larger than 0.8); (<b>3</b>) <b>Platanus</b>: version 1.24, default parameters, minimum number of link was 4 for scaffolding; (<b>4</b>) <b>Krskgf</b>: version 1.2, default parameters; (<b>5</b>) <b>GapCloser</b>: version 1.12, default parameters; (<b>6</b>) <b>Pbjelly</b>: version 15.8.24, default parameters; (<b>7</b>) <b>Haplomerger</b>: version 20161205, default parameters, ââstepâ=â20â to speed up the LASTZ process; âminOverlapâ=â99,999,999â to avoid mixing up haplotypes; (<b>8</b>) <b>Augustus</b>: version 3.0.3, parameters: âgff3â=âon, âspeciesâ=âteaplant, âgenemodelâ=âpartial, âUTRâ=âoff, âuniqueGeneIdâ=âtrue; (<b>9</b>) <b>Blastall</b>: version 2.2.26, parameters: -e 1e-5, -F F; (<b>10</b>) <b>GeneWise</b>: version 2.2.0, parameters: -genesf -quiet âpseudo; (<b>11</b>) <b>PASA</b>: version 2.0.2, parameters: -C; â-Râ to run alignment/assembly pipeline; â-Tâ to indicate that transcripts were trimmed using the TGI seqclean tool; (<b>12</b>) <b>StringTie</b>: version 1.3.1b, default parameters; (<b>13</b>) <b>MAKER</b>: version 2.31.8, parameters: organism_typeâ=âeukaryotic, min_contigâ=â150, min_proteinâ=â50, alt_spliceâ=â1, correct_est_fusionâ=â1; (<b>14</b>) <b>InterProScan</b>: version 5.3â46.0, parameters:âdisable-precalc,âgoterms,âpathways; (<b>15</b>) <b>RepeatModeler</b>: version 1.0.11, parameters: -database teaplant, -e ncbi, -pa 30; <b>16</b>) <b>LTR_Finder</b>: version 1.07, default parameters; (<b>17</b>) <b>Repeatmasker</b>: version 4.0.5, parameters: -e ncbi, -pa 30; (<b>18</b>) <b>RepeatProteinMask</b>: version 4.0.5, default parameters; (<b>19</b>) <b>Tandem Repeats Finder</b>: version 4.04, default parameters; (<b>20</b>) <b>BUSCO</b>: version 2.0, default parameters, the lineage dataset is: embryophyta_odb9 (Creation date: 2016-11-01, number of species: 30, number of BUSCOs: 1440); (<b>21</b>) <b>Primer premier</b>: version 5.0.</p>
